Multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL) tackles sequential decision-making problems where multiple players simultaneously interact with the shared environment, affecting each other’s behavior in a coupled manner. Under a competitive reinforcement learning (RL) setting, the goal of each player is to maximize (resp. minimize) her own cumulative gains (resp. losses) in the presence of other agents. Recent years have been tremendous practical successes of MARL in a variety of application domains, such as autonomous driving (Shalev-Shwartz et al., 2016), Go (Silver et al., 2016), StarCraft (Vinyals et al., 2017), Dota2 (Berner et al., 2019) and Poker (Brown & Sandholm, 2019).These successes are attributed to advanced MARL algorithms that can coordinate multiple players by exploiting potentially partial observations of the latent states and employ powerful function approximators (neural networks in particular), which empower us to tackle practical problems with large state spaces.

Apart from the empirical success, there is a growing body of literature on establishing theoretical guarantees for Markov games (MGs) (Shapley, 1953) – a standard framework for describing the dynamics of competitive RL.In particular, Xie et al. (2020); Chen et al. (2022d); Jin et al. (2021c); Huang et al. (2021); Zhan et al. (2022a) extend the works in single-agent reinforcement learning (RL) with function approximation (Jiang et al., 2017; Sun et al., 2019; Wang et al., 2020a; Jin et al., 2020b; Ayoub et al., 2020; Cai et al., 2020; Dann et al., 2021; Jin et al., 2021a; Du et al., 2021) by developing sample-efficient algorithms that are capable to solve two-player zero-sum MGs with function approximation. In addition, as opposed to the aforementioned literature on MGs assuming the state of players is fully observable, the recent work Liu et al. (2022c) analyze Markov games under partial observability (Liu et al., 2022c), i.e., the complete information about underlying states is lacking. However, most of the existing works are built upon the principle of “optimism in the face of uncertainty” (OFU) (Lattimore & Szepesvári, 2020) for exploration.Furthermore, from a practical perspective, achieving optimism often requires explicit construction of bonus functions, which are often designed in a model-specific fashion and computationally challenging to implement.

Another promising strand of exploration techniques is based on posterior sampling, which is shown by previous works on bandits (Chapelle & Li, 2011) and RL (Osband et al., 2016) to perform better than OFU-based algorithms. Meanwhile, posterior sampling methods, unlike OFU-based algorithms (Jin et al., 2021a; Du et al., 2021) that need to solve complex optimization problems to achieve optimism, can be efficiently implemented by ensemble approximations (Osband et al., 2016; Lu & Van Roy, 2017; Chua et al., 2018; Nagabandi et al., 2020) and stochastic gradient Langevin dynamics (SGLD) (Welling & Teh, 2011). Despite the superiority of posterior sampling, its theoretical understanding in MARL remains limited.The only exception is Xiong et al. (2022b), which proposes a model-free posterior sampling algorithm for zero-sum MGs with general function approximation. However, Xiong et al. (2022b) cannot capture some common tractable competitive RL modelswith a model-based nature, such aslinear mixture MGs (Chen et al., 2022d) and low witness rank MGs (Huang et al., 2021).Moreover, their result is restricted to the fully observable MGs without handling the partial observability of the players’ states. Therefore, we raise the following question:

Can we design provably sample-efficient posterior sampling algorithms for competitive RL with even partial observations under general function approximation?

Concretely, the above question poses three major challenges. First, despite the success of the OFU principle in partially observable Markov games (POMGs), it remains elusive how to incorporate the partial observations into the posterior sampling framework under a MARL setting with provably efficient exploration. Second, it is also unclear whether there is a generic function approximation condition that can cover more known classes in both full and partial observable MARL and is meanwhile compatible with the posterior sampling framework. Third, with the partial observation and function approximation, it is challenging to explore how we can solve MGs under the setups of self-play, where all players can be coordinated together, and adversarial learning, where the opponents’ policies are adversarial and uncontrollable by the learner.Our work takes an initial step towards tackling such challenges by concentrating on the typical competitive RL scenario, the two-player zero-sum MG, and proposing statistically efficient posterior sampling algorithms under function approximation that can solve both self-play and adversarial MGs with full and partial observations.

Contributions. Our contributions are four-fold: (1) We first propose the two generalized eluder coefficient (GEC) as the complexity measure for the competitive RL with function approximation, namely self-play GEC and adversarial GEC, that captures the exploration-exploitation tradeoff in many existing MGs, including linear MGs, linear mixture MGs, weakly revealing POMGs, decodable POMGs. The proposed measures also generalize the recently developed GEC condition (Zhong et al., 2022b) from single-agent RL to MARL, suitably adjusting the exploration policy particularly for the adversarial setting. (2) Incorporating the proposed self-play GEC for general function approximation, we propose a model-based posterior sampling algorithm with self-play to learn the Nash equilibrium (NE), which successfully handles the partial observability of states along with a full observable setup by carefully designed likelihood functions. (3) We identify POMG models aligned with the form of the adversarial GEC, which fit MG learning with adversarially-varying policies of the opponent. We further propose a model-based posterior sampling algorithm for adversarial learning with general function approximation.(4) We prove regret bounds for our proposed algorithms that scale sublinearly with the number of episodes T𝑇T, the corresponding GEC dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}}, and a quantity measuring the coverage of the optimal model by the initial model sampling distribution.To the best of our knowledge, we present the first model-based posterior sampling approaches to sample-efficiently learn MGs with function approximation, handling partial observability in both self-play and adversarial settings.

Related Works.There is a large body of literature studying MGs, especially zero-sum MGs. In the self-play setting, many papers have focused on solving approximate NE in tabular zero-sum MGs (Bai & Jin, 2020; Bai et al., 2020; Xie et al., 2020; Qiu et al., 2021a; Liu et al., 2021), zero-sum MGs with linear function approximation (Xie et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2022d), zero-sum MGs with low-rank structures (Qiu et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2022; Ni et al., 2022), and zero-sum MGs with general function approximation (Jin et al., 2021c; Huang et al., 2021; Xiong et al., 2022b).On the other hand, there are also several recent papers focusing on the adversarial setting (Xie et al., 2020; Tian et al., 2021; Jin et al., 2021c; Huang et al., 2021) aim to learn the Nash value under the setting of unrevealed opponent’s policies, where the adversarial policies of the opponent are unobservable.In addition, another line of adversarial MGs concentrates on a revealed policy setting, where the opponent’s full policy can be observed, leading to efficiently learning a sublinear regret comparing against the best policy in hindsight. Particularly, Liu et al. (2022d) and Zhan et al. (2022a) develop efficient algorithms in tabular and function approximation settings, respectively. Our approach focuses on the unrevealed policy setting, which is considered to be a more practical setup.There are also works studying MGs from various aspects (Song et al., 2021; Jin et al., 2021b; Mao & Başar, 2021; Zhong et al., 2023; Jin et al., 2022; Daskalakis et al., 2022; Qiu et al., 2021b; Blanchet et al., 2023; Zhong et al., 2022a; Cui & Du, 2022; Xiong et al., 2022a; Yang et al., 2023), such as multi-player general-sum MGs, reward-free MGs, MGs with delayed feedback, and offline MGs, which are beyond the scope of our work. Most of the aforementioned works follow the OFU principle and differ from our posterior sampling methods.The recent work Xiong et al. (2022b) proposes a model-free posterior sampling algorithm for two-player zero-sum MGs but is limited to the self-play setting with fully observable states. Moreover, their work requires a strong Bellman completeness assumption that is restrictive compared to only requiring realizability in our work, mainly due to the monotonicity, meaning that adding a new function to the function class may violate it. Many model-based models like linear mixture MGs (Chen et al., 2022d) and low witness rank MGs (Huang et al., 2021) are not Bellman-complete, so they cannot be captured by Xiong et al. (2022b). Without the completeness assumption, our model-based posterior sampling approaches can solve a rich class of tractable MGs, including linear mixture MGs, low witness rank MGs (Huang et al., 2021), and even POMGs, tackling both self-play and adversarial learning settings.

Our work is related to a line of research on posterior sampling methods in RL. For single-agent RL, most existing works such as Russo & Van Roy (2014) analyze the Bayesian regret bound. There are also some works (Agrawal & Jia, 2017; Russo, 2019; Zanette et al., 2020) focusing on the frequentist (worst-case) regret bound.Our work is more closely related to the recently developed feel-good Thompson sampling technique proposed by Zhang (2022) for the frequentist regret bound, and its extension to single-agent RL (Dann et al., 2021; Agarwal & Zhang, 2022a, b) and two-player zero-sum MGs (Xiong et al., 2022b).

Our work is also closely related to the line of research on function approximation in RL. Such a line of works proposes algorithms for efficient policy learning under diverse function approximation classes, spanning from linear Markov decision processes (MDPs) (Jin et al., 2020b), linear mixture MDPs (Ayoub et al., 2020; Zhou et al., 2021) to nonlinear and general function classes, including, for instance, generalized linear MDPs (Wang et al., 2019), kernel and neural function classes (Yang et al., 2020), bounded eluder dimension (Osband & Van Roy, 2014; Wang et al., 2020b), Bellman rank (Jiang et al., 2017), witness rank (Sun et al., 2019), bellman eluder dimension (Jin et al., 2021a), bilinear (Du et al., 2021), decision-estimation coefficient (Foster et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2022b), decoupling coefficient (Dann et al., 2021), admissible Bellman characterization (Chen et al., 2022c), and GEC (Zhong et al., 2022b) classes.

The research on partial observability in RL (Guo et al., 2016) is closely related to our work. The works Krishnamurthy et al. (2016); Jin et al. (2020a) show that learning history-dependent policies generally can cause an exponential sample complexity. Thus, many recent works focus on analyzing tractable subclasses of partially observable Markov decision processes (POMDPs), which includes weakly revealing POMDPs (Jin et al., 2020a; Liu et al., 2022a), observable POMDPs (Golowich et al., 2022b, a), decodable POMDPs (Du et al., 2019; Efroni et al., 2022), low-rank POMDPs(Wang et al., ), regular PSR (Zhan et al., 2022b), PO-bilinear class (Uehara et al., 2022b), latent MDP with sufficient tests (Kwon et al., 2021), B-stable PSR (Chen et al., 2022a), well-conditioned PSR (Liu et al., 2022b), POMDPs with deterministic transition kernels (Jin et al., 2020a; Uehara et al., 2022a), and GEC (Zhong et al., 2022b).Nevertheless, in contrast to our work which focuses on the two-player competitive setting with partial observation, these papers merely consider the single-agent setting.The recent research Liu et al. (2022c) further generalizes weakly revealing POMDPs to its multi-agent counterpart, weakly revealing POMGs, in a general-sum multi-player setting based on the OFU principle. But when specialized to the two-player case, our work proposes a general function class that can subsume the class of weakly revealing POMGs as a special case. It would be intriguing to generalize our framework to the general-sum settings in the future.

We introduce the basic concept of the two-player zero-sum Markov game (MG), function approximation, and the new complexity conditions for function approximation. Concretely, we study two typical classes of MGs, i.e., fully observable MGs and partially observable MGs, as defined below.

Fully Observable Markov Game. We consider an episodic two-player zero-sum fully observable Markov game (FOMG333FOMG is typically referred to as MG by most literature. We adopt FOMG to differentiate it from POMG.) specified by a tuple (𝒮,𝒜,ℬ,ℙ,r,H)𝒮𝒜ℬℙ𝑟𝐻({\mathcal{S}},\mathcal{A},\mathcal{B},\mathbb{P},r,H), where 𝒮𝒮{\mathcal{S}} is the state space, 𝒜𝒜\mathcal{A} and ℬℬ\mathcal{B} are the action spaces of Players 1 and 2 respectively, H𝐻H is the length of the episode. Online Interaction with the Environment.In POMGs, at step hℎh of episode t𝑡t of the interaction, players take actions aht∼πht(⋅|τh−1t,oht)a_{h}^{t}\sim\pi_{h}^{t}(\cdot|\tau_{h-1}^{t},o_{h}^{t}) and bht∼νht(⋅|τh−1t,bh−1t,oht)b_{h}^{t}\sim\nu_{h}^{t}(\cdot|\tau_{h-1}^{t},b_{h-1}^{t},o_{h}^{t}) depending on their action and observation histories, receiving a reward rh​(oht,aht,bht)subscript𝑟ℎsuperscriptsubscript𝑜ℎ𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑎ℎ𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑏ℎ𝑡r_{h}(o_{h}^{t},a_{h}^{t},b_{h}^{t}) and transitions from the latent state shtsuperscriptsubscript𝑠ℎ𝑡s_{h}^{t} to sh+1t∼ℙh(⋅|sht,aht,bht)s_{h+1}^{t}\sim\mathbb{P}_{h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s_{h}^{t},a_{h}^{t},b_{h}^{t}) with an observation oh+1t∼𝕆h(⋅|sht)o_{h+1}^{t}\sim\mathbb{O}_{h}(\cdot|s_{h}^{t}) generated. Value Function, Best Response, and Nash Equilibrium. To characterize the learning objective and the performance of the algorithms, we define the value function as the expected cumulative rewards under the policy pair (π,ν)𝜋𝜈(\pi,\nu) starting from the initial step h=1ℎ1h=1. For FOMGs, we define the value function asVπ,ν:=𝔼​[∑h=1Hrh​(sh,ah,bh)|s1,π,ν,ℙ]assignsuperscript𝑉𝜋𝜈𝔼delimited-[]conditionalsuperscriptsubscriptℎ1𝐻subscript𝑟ℎsubscript𝑠ℎsubscript𝑎ℎsubscript𝑏ℎsubscript𝑠1𝜋𝜈ℙV^{\pi,\nu}:=\mathbb{E}[\sum_{h=1}^{H}r_{h}(s_{h},a_{h},b_{h})~{}|~{}s_{1},\pi,\nu,\mathbb{P}], where the expectation is taken over all the randomness induced by π𝜋\pi, ν𝜈\nu, and ℙℙ\mathbb{P}. For POMG, we define the value function asVπ,ν:=𝔼​[∑h=1Hrh​(oh,ah,bh)|π,ν,θ]assignsuperscript𝑉𝜋𝜈𝔼delimited-[]conditionalsuperscriptsubscriptℎ1𝐻subscript𝑟ℎsubscript𝑜ℎsubscript𝑎ℎsubscript𝑏ℎ𝜋𝜈𝜃V^{\pi,\nu}:=\mathbb{E}[\sum_{h=1}^{H}r_{h}(o_{h},a_{h},b_{h})~{}|\pi,\nu,\theta], with the expectation taken for π𝜋\pi, ν𝜈\nu, and θ𝜃\theta.

Our work studies the competitive setting of RL, where Player 1 (max-player) aims to maximize the value function Vπ,νsuperscript𝑉𝜋𝜈V^{\pi,\nu} while Player 2 (min-player) aims to minimize it. With the defined value function, given a policy pair (π,ν)𝜋𝜈(\pi,\nu), we define their best responses respectively as br​(π)∈arg⁡minν⁡Vπ,νbr𝜋subscript𝜈superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈\texttt{br}(\pi)\in\arg\min_{\nu}V^{\pi,\nu} and br​(ν)∈arg⁡maxπ⁡Vπ,νbr𝜈subscript𝜋superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈\texttt{br}(\nu)\in\arg\max_{\pi}V^{\pi,\nu}.Then, we say a policy pair (π∗,ν∗)superscript𝜋superscript𝜈(\pi^{*},\nu^{*}) is a Nash equilibrium (NE) ifVπ∗,ν∗=maxπ⁡minν⁡Vπ,ν=minν⁡maxπ⁡Vπ,ν.superscript𝑉superscript𝜋superscript𝜈subscript𝜋subscript𝜈superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈subscript𝜈subscript𝜋superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈\displaystyle V^{\pi^{*},\nu^{*}}=\max_{\pi}\min_{\nu}V^{\pi,\nu}=\min_{\nu}\max_{\pi}V^{\pi,\nu}.Thus, it always holds that π∗=br​(ν∗)superscript𝜋brsuperscript𝜈\pi^{*}=\texttt{br}(\nu^{*}) and ν∗=br​(π∗)superscript𝜈brsuperscript𝜋\nu^{*}=\texttt{br}(\pi^{*}). For abbreviation, we denote V∗=Vπ∗,ν∗superscript𝑉superscript𝑉superscript𝜋superscript𝜈V^{*}=V^{\pi^{*},\nu^{*}}, Vπ,∗=minν⁡Vπ,νsuperscript𝑉𝜋subscript𝜈superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈V^{\pi,*}=\min_{\nu}V^{\pi,\nu}, and V∗,ν=maxπ⁡Vπ,νsuperscript𝑉𝜈subscript𝜋superscript𝑉𝜋𝜈V^{*,\nu}=\max_{\pi}V^{\pi,\nu}, which implies V∗=Vπ∗,∗=V∗,ν∗superscript𝑉superscript𝑉superscript𝜋superscript𝑉superscript𝜈V^{*}=V^{\pi^{*},*}=V^{*,\nu^{*}} for NE (π∗,ν∗)superscript𝜋superscript𝜈(\pi^{*},\nu^{*}). Moreover, we define the policy pair (π,ν)𝜋𝜈(\pi,\nu) as an ε𝜀\varepsilon-approximate NE if it satisfies V∗,ν−Vπ,∗≤εsuperscript𝑉𝜈superscript𝑉𝜋𝜀V^{*,\nu}-V^{\pi,*}\leq\varepsilon.

Function Approximation.Since the environment is unknown to players, the model-based RL setting requires us to learn the true model of the environment, f∗superscript𝑓f^{*}, via (general) function approximation. We use the functions f𝑓f lying in a general model function class ℱℱ\mathcal{F} to approximate the environment. We make a standard realizability assumption on the relationship between the model class and the true model.

In our work, the true model f∗superscript𝑓f^{*} represents the transition kernel ℙℙ\mathbb{P} for the FOMG and θ𝜃\theta for the POMG. For any f∈ℱ𝑓ℱf\in\mathcal{F}, we let ℙfsubscriptℙ𝑓\mathbb{P}_{f} and θf=(μf,ℙf,𝕆f)subscript𝜃𝑓subscript𝜇𝑓subscriptℙ𝑓subscript𝕆𝑓\theta_{f}=(\mu_{f},\mathbb{P}_{f},\mathbb{O}_{f}) be the models under the approximation function f𝑓f and Vfπ,νsuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓𝜋𝜈V_{f}^{\pi,\nu} the value function associated with f𝑓f. For POMGs, we denote 𝐏f,hπ,νsuperscriptsubscript𝐏𝑓ℎ𝜋𝜈\mathbf{P}_{f,h}^{\pi,\nu} and 𝐏f,hsubscript𝐏𝑓ℎ\mathbf{P}_{f,h} as 𝐏hπ,νsubscriptsuperscript𝐏𝜋𝜈ℎ\mathbf{P}^{\pi,\nu}_{h} and 𝐏hsubscript𝐏ℎ\mathbf{P}_{h} under the model f𝑓f.

MGs with Self-Play and Adversarial Learning.Our work investigates two important MG setups for competitive RL, which are the self-play setting and the adversarial setting. In the self-play setting, the learner can control both players together to execute the proposed algorithms to learn an approximate NE. Therefore, our objective is to design sample-efficient algorithms to generate a sequence of policy pairs {(πt,νt)}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡superscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{(\pi^{t},\nu^{t})\}_{t=1}^{T} in T𝑇T episodes such that the following regret can be minimized,Regsp​(T):=∑t=1T[Vf∗∗,νt−Vf∗πt,∗].assignsuperscriptRegsp𝑇superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇delimited-[]superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓superscript𝜈𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓superscript𝜋𝑡\displaystyle\mathrm{Reg}^{\mathrm{sp}}(T):=\textstyle\sum_{t=1}^{T}\big{[}V_{f^{*}}^{*,\nu^{t}}-V_{f^{*}}^{\pi^{t},*}\big{]}.In the adversarial setting, we can no longer coordinate both players, and only single player is controllable. Under such a circumstance, the opponent plays arbitrary and even adversarial policies. Wlog, suppose that the main player is the max-player with the policies {πt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\pi^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} generated by a carefully designed algorithm and the opponent is min-player with arbitrary policies {νt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\nu^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T}. The objective of the algorithm is to learn policies {πt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\pi^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} to maximize the overall cumulative rewards in the presence of an adversary.To measure the performance of algorithms, we define the following regret for the adversarial setting by comparing the learned value against the Nash value, i.e.,Regadv​(T)=∑t=1T[Vf∗∗−Vf∗πt,νt].superscriptRegadv𝑇superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇delimited-[]subscriptsuperscript𝑉superscript𝑓subscriptsuperscript𝑉superscript𝜋𝑡superscript𝜈𝑡superscript𝑓\displaystyle\mathrm{Reg}^{\mathrm{adv}}(T)=\textstyle\sum_{t=1}^{T}\big{[}V^{*}_{f^{*}}-V^{\pi^{t},\nu^{t}}_{f^{*}}\big{]}.

We propose algorithms aiming to generate a sequence of policy pairs {(πt,νt)}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡superscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{(\pi^{t},\nu^{t})\}_{t=1}^{T} by controlling the learning process of both players such that the regret Regsp​(T)superscriptRegsp𝑇\mathrm{Reg}^{\mathrm{sp}}(T) is small. Such a regret can be decomposed into two parts, namely ∑t=1T[Vf∗∗−Vf∗πt,∗]superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇delimited-[]superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓superscript𝜋𝑡\sum_{t=1}^{T}[V_{f^{*}}^{*}-V_{f^{*}}^{\pi^{t},*}] and ∑t=1T[Vf∗∗,νt−Vf∗∗]superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇delimited-[]superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓superscript𝜈𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓\sum_{t=1}^{T}[V_{f^{*}}^{*,\nu^{t}}-V_{f^{*}}^{*}], which inspires our to design algorithms for learning {πt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\pi^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} and {νt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\nu^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} separately by targeting at minimizing these two parts respectively. Due to the symmetric structure of such a game learning problem, we propose the algorithm to learn {πt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜋𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\pi^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} as summarized in Algorithm 1. The algorithm for learning {νt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\nu^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} can be proposed in a symmetric way in Algorithm 3, which is deferred to Appendix 6.Our proposed algorithm features an integration of the model-based posterior sampling and the exploiter-guided self-play in a multi-agent learning scenario. In Algorithm 1, Player 1 is the main player, while Player 2 is called the exploiter, who assists the learning of the main player by exploiting her weakness.

Posterior Sampling for the Main Player. The posterior sampling constructs a posterior distribution pt(⋅|Zt−1)p^{t}(\cdot|Z^{t-1}) over the function class ℱℱ\mathcal{F} each round based on collected data and a pre-specified prior distribution p0​(⋅)superscript𝑝0⋅p^{0}(\cdot), where Zt−1superscript𝑍𝑡1Z^{t-1} denotes the random history up to the end of the (t−1)𝑡1(t-1)-th episode. For ease of notation, hereafter, we omit Zt−1superscript𝑍𝑡1Z^{t-1} in the posterior distribution. Most recent literature shows that adding an optimism term in the posterior distribution can lead to sample-efficient RL algorithms. Thereby, we define the distribution pt​(⋅)superscript𝑝𝑡⋅p^{t}(\cdot) over the function class ℱℱ\mathcal{F} for the main player as in Line 3 of Algorithm 1, which is proportional to p0​(f)​exp⁡[γ​Vf∗+∑τ=1t−1∑h=1HLhτ​(f)]superscript𝑝0𝑓𝛾superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓superscriptsubscript𝜏1𝑡1superscriptsubscriptℎ1𝐻subscriptsuperscript𝐿𝜏ℎ𝑓p^{0}(f)\exp[\gamma V_{f}^{*}+\sum_{\tau=1}^{t-1}\sum_{h=1}^{H}L^{\tau}_{h}(f)]. Here, γ​Vf∗𝛾superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓\gamma V_{f}^{*} serves as the optimism term, and Lhτ​(f)subscriptsuperscript𝐿𝜏ℎ𝑓L^{\tau}_{h}(f) is the likelihood function built upon the pre-collected data. Such a construction of pt​(⋅)superscript𝑝𝑡⋅p^{t}(\cdot) indicates that we will assign a higher probability (density) to a function f𝑓f, which results in higher values of the combination of the optimism term and the likelihood function. We sample a model f¯tsuperscript¯𝑓𝑡\overline{f}^{t} from the distribution pt​(⋅)superscript𝑝𝑡⋅p^{t}(\cdot) over the model class and learn the policy πtsuperscript𝜋𝑡\pi^{t} for the main player such that (πt,ν¯t)superscript𝜋𝑡superscript¯𝜈𝑡(\pi^{t},\overline{\nu}^{t}) is the NE of the value function under the model f¯tsuperscript¯𝑓𝑡\overline{f}^{t} in Line 4, where ν¯tsuperscript¯𝜈𝑡\overline{\nu}^{t} is a dummy policy and only used in our theoretical analysis.

Posterior Sampling for the Exploiter. The exploiter aims to track the best response of πtsuperscript𝜋𝑡\pi^{t} to assist learning a low regret. The best response of πtsuperscript𝜋𝑡\pi^{t} generated by the exploiter is nevertheless based on a value function under a different model than f¯tsuperscript¯𝑓𝑡\overline{f}^{t}. Specifically, for the exploiter, we define the posterior sampling distribution qt​(⋅)superscript𝑞𝑡⋅q^{t}(\cdot) using an optimism term −γ​Vfπt,∗𝛾superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓superscript𝜋𝑡-\gamma V_{f}^{\pi^{t},*} and the summation of likelihood functions, i.e., ∑τ=1t−1∑h=1HLhτ​(f)superscriptsubscript𝜏1𝑡1superscriptsubscriptℎ1𝐻subscriptsuperscript𝐿𝜏ℎ𝑓\sum_{\tau=1}^{t-1}\sum_{h=1}^{H}L^{\tau}_{h}(f), along with a prior distribution q0​(⋅)superscript𝑞0⋅q^{0}(\cdot), in Line 5 of Algorithm 1. The negative term −γ​Vfπt,∗𝛾superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓superscript𝜋𝑡-\gamma V_{f}^{\pi^{t},*} favors a model with a low value and is thus optimistic from the exploiter’s perspective but pessimistic for the main player. We then sample a model f¯tsuperscript¯𝑓𝑡\underline{f}^{t} from qt​(⋅)superscript𝑞𝑡⋅q^{t}(\cdot) and compute the best response of πtsuperscript𝜋𝑡\pi^{t}, denoted as ν¯tsuperscript¯𝜈𝑡\underline{\nu}^{t}, under the model f¯tsuperscript¯𝑓𝑡\underline{f}^{t} as in Line 7.

Our regret analysis is based on a novel structural complexity condition for multi-agent RL and a quantity to measure how the well the prior distributions cover the optimal model f∗superscript𝑓f^{*}. We first define the following condition for the self-play setting.

Our definition of self-play GEC is inspired by Zhong et al. (2022b) for the single-agent RL. Then, it shares an analogous meaning to the single-agent GEC. Here (στ,h)superscript𝜎𝜏ℎ(\sigma^{\tau},h) implies running the joint exploration policy στsuperscript𝜎𝜏\sigma^{\tau} to step hℎh to collect a data point ξhτsuperscriptsubscript𝜉ℎ𝜏\xi_{h}^{\tau}. The LHS of the inequality is viewed as the prediction error and the RHS is the training error defined on a loss function ℓℓ\ell plus a burn-in error 2​H​(d​H​T)12+ϵ​H​T2𝐻superscript𝑑𝐻𝑇12italic-ϵ𝐻𝑇2H(dHT)^{\frac{1}{2}}+\epsilon HT that is non-dominating when ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon is small. The loss function ℓℓ\ell and ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon can be problem-specific. We determine ℓ​(f,ξh)ℓ𝑓subscript𝜉ℎ\ell(f,\xi_{h}) for FOMGs with ξh=(sh,ah)subscript𝜉ℎsubscript𝑠ℎsubscript𝑎ℎ\xi_{h}=(s_{h},a_{h}) and POMGs with ξh=τhsubscript𝜉ℎsubscript𝜏ℎ\xi_{h}=\tau_{h} respectively asFOMG:DHe2(ℙf,h(⋅|ξh),ℙf∗,h(⋅|ξh)),POMG:1/2⋅(𝐏f,h​(ξh)/𝐏f∗,h​(ξh)−1)2,\displaystyle\text{FOMG:}~{}~{}D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbb{P}_{f,h}(\cdot|\xi_{h}),\mathbb{P}_{f^{*},h}(\cdot|\xi_{h})),\quad\text{POMG:}~{}~{}1/2\cdot\Big{(}\sqrt{\mathbf{P}_{f,h}(\xi_{h})/\mathbf{P}_{f^{*},h}(\xi_{h})}-1\Big{)}^{2},(3)such that 𝔼(σ,h)​[ℓ​(f,ξh)]=DHe2​(𝐏f∗,hσ,𝐏f,hσ)subscript𝔼𝜎ℎdelimited-[]ℓ𝑓subscript𝜉ℎsuperscriptsubscript𝐷He2superscriptsubscript𝐏superscript𝑓ℎ𝜎superscriptsubscript𝐏𝑓ℎ𝜎\mathbb{E}_{(\sigma,h)}[\ell(f,\xi_{h})]=D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbf{P}_{f^{*},h}^{\sigma},\mathbf{P}_{f,h}^{\sigma}) for POMGs. The intuition for GEC is that if hypotheses have a small training error on a well-explored dataset, then the out-of-sample prediction error is also small, which characterizes the hardness of environment exploration.

Since the posterior sampling steps in our algorithms depend on the initial distributions p0superscript𝑝0p^{0} and q0superscript𝑞0q^{0}, we define the following quantity to measure howwell the prior distributions p0superscript𝑝0p^{0} and q0superscript𝑞0q^{0} cover the optimal model f∗∈ℱsuperscript𝑓ℱf^{*}\in\mathcal{F}, which is also a multi-agent generalization of its single-agent version (Agarwal & Zhang, 2022a; Zhong et al., 2022b).

When the model class ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is a finite space, if let p0=Unif​(ℱ)superscript𝑝0Unifℱp^{0}=\mathrm{Unif}(\mathcal{F}), we simply know that ω​(β,p0)≤log⁡|ℱ|𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0ℱ\omega(\beta,p^{0})\leq\log|\mathcal{F}| where |ℱ|ℱ|\mathcal{F}| is the cardinality of ℱℱ\mathcal{F}. Furthermore, for an infinite function class ℱℱ\mathcal{F}, the term log⁡|ℱ|ℱ\log|\mathcal{F}| can be substituted by a quantity having logarithmic dependence on the covering number of the function class ℱℱ\mathcal{F}. The above results indicate that the proposed posterior sampling self-play algorithms (Algorithms 1 and 3) separately admit a sublinear dependence on GEC dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}}, the number of learning episodes T𝑇T, as well as ω​(4​H​T,p0)𝜔4𝐻𝑇superscript𝑝0\omega(4HT,p^{0}) and ω​(4​H​T,q0)𝜔4𝐻𝑇superscript𝑞0\omega(4HT,q^{0}) for both FOMG and POMG settings. The quantity ω𝜔\omega can be associated with the log-covering number if ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is infinite. Please see Appendix 8 for analysis.

Without loss of generality, we assume that the max-player is the main agent and the min-player is the opponent. Under this setting, the goal of the main player is to maximize her cumulative rewards as much as possible, comparing against the value under the NE, i.e., Vf∗∗superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓V_{f^{*}}^{*}.We develop a novel algorithm for this setting as summarized in Algorithm 2. In our algorithm, the opponent’s policy is assumed to be arbitrary and is also not revealed to the main player. The only information about the opponent is the current state or the partial observation of her state as well as the actions taken.

We adopt the optimistic posterior sampling approach for the main player with defining an optimism term as γ​Vf∗𝛾superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓\gamma V_{f}^{*} motivated by the above learning target, and the likelihood function Lht​(f)superscriptsubscript𝐿ℎ𝑡𝑓L_{h}^{t}(f) with Lht​(f):=η​log⁡ℙf,h​(sh+1t|sht,aht,bht)assignsubscriptsuperscript𝐿𝑡ℎ𝑓𝜂subscriptℙ𝑓ℎconditionalsuperscriptsubscript𝑠ℎ1𝑡subscriptsuperscript𝑠𝑡ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝑎𝑡ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝑏𝑡ℎL^{t}_{h}(f):=\eta\log\mathbb{P}_{f,h}(s_{h+1}^{t}{\,|\,}s^{t}_{h},a^{t}_{h},b^{t}_{h}) in (1) for FOMGs and Lht​(f)=η​log⁡𝐏f,h​(τht)subscriptsuperscript𝐿𝑡ℎ𝑓𝜂subscript𝐏𝑓ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝜏𝑡ℎL^{t}_{h}(f)=\eta\log\mathbf{P}_{f,h}(\tau^{t}_{h}) in (2) for POMGs respectively. The policy πtsuperscript𝜋𝑡\pi^{t} learned by the main player is from computing the NE of the value function under the current model ftsuperscript𝑓𝑡f^{t} sampled from the posterior distribution ptsuperscript𝑝𝑡p^{t}. In addition, the joint exploration policy is set to be σt=(πt,νt)superscript𝜎𝑡superscript𝜋𝑡superscript𝜈𝑡\sigma^{t}=(\pi^{t},\nu^{t}) where νtsuperscript𝜈𝑡\nu^{t} is the potentially adversarial policy played by the opponent. Thus, we can collect the data defined as 𝒟t={(sht,aht,bht,sh+1t)}h=1Hsuperscript𝒟𝑡superscriptsubscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑠𝑡ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝑎𝑡ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝑏𝑡ℎsubscriptsuperscript𝑠𝑡ℎ1ℎ1𝐻\mathcal{D}^{t}=\{(s^{t}_{h},a^{t}_{h},b^{t}_{h},s^{t}_{h+1})\}_{h=1}^{H} and 𝒟t={τht}h=1Hsuperscript𝒟𝑡superscriptsubscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝜏ℎ𝑡ℎ1𝐻\mathcal{D}^{t}=\{\tau_{h}^{t}\}_{h=1}^{H} with τht=(o1t,a1t,b1t​…,oht,aht,bht)superscriptsubscript𝜏ℎ𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑜1𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑎1𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑏1𝑡…superscriptsubscript𝑜ℎ𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑎ℎ𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑏ℎ𝑡\tau_{h}^{t}=(o_{1}^{t},a_{1}^{t},b_{1}^{t}\ldots,o_{h}^{t},a_{h}^{t},b_{h}^{t}) for FOMGs and POMGs respectively, collected by executing σtsuperscript𝜎𝑡\sigma^{t} to the hℎh-th step of the game for each h∈[H]ℎdelimited-[]𝐻h\in[H].

Before demonstrating our regret analysis, we first define a multi-agent GEC fitting the adversarial learning scenario. Considering that the opponent’s policy is uncontrollable during the learning, we let {νt}t=1Tsuperscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝜈𝑡𝑡1𝑇\{\nu^{t}\}_{t=1}^{T} be arbitrary, which is clearly distinguished from self-play GEC defined in Definition 3.1.

Our regret analysis for Algorithm 2 also depends on the quantity ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) that characterizes the coverage of the prior distribution p0superscript𝑝0p^{0} on the true model f∗superscript𝑓f^{*}. Then, we have the following regret bound.

The above result indicates that we can achieve a meaningful regret bound by a posterior sampling algorithm with general function approximation, even when the opponent’s policy is adversarial and her full policies νtsuperscript𝜈𝑡\nu^{t} are not revealed. This regret has a sublinear dependence on dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}}, the number of episodes T𝑇T, as well as ω​(4​H​T,p0)𝜔4𝐻𝑇superscript𝑝0\omega(4HT,p^{0}). Similarly, when ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is finite, Algorithm 2 admits a regret of O​(dGEC​H​T⋅log⁡|ℱ|)𝑂⋅subscript𝑑GEC𝐻𝑇ℱO(\sqrt{d_{\mathrm{GEC}}HT\cdot\log|\mathcal{F}|}). The term log⁡|ℱ|ℱ\log|\mathcal{F}| can be the log-covering number of ℱℱ\mathcal{F} if it is infinite.

This section presents several examples of tractable MG classes captured by the self-play and adversarial GEC, the discussion of the quantity ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}), and the proof sketches of main theorems.

Examples. We call the class with a low dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}} the low self-play GEC class and low adversarial GEC class. Next, we analyze the relation between the proposed classes and the following MG classes. We also propose a new decodable POMG class generalized from the single-agent POMDP. We note that except for the linear MG, the other classes cannot be analyzed by the recent posterior sampling work Xiong et al. (2022b). We defer detailed definitions and proofs to Appendix 7.•Linear MG. This FOMG class admits a linear structure of the reward and transition by feature vectors ϕ​(s,a,b)∈ℝdbold-italic-ϕ𝑠𝑎𝑏superscriptℝ𝑑\bm{\phi}(s,a,b)\in\mathbb{R}^{d} as rh​(s,a,b)=𝐰h⊤​ϕ​(s,a,b)subscript𝑟ℎ𝑠𝑎𝑏superscriptsubscript𝐰ℎtopbold-italic-ϕ𝑠𝑎𝑏r_{h}(s,a,b)=\mathbf{w}_{h}^{\top}\bm{\phi}(s,a,b) and ℙh​(s′|s,a,b)=𝜽h​(s′)⊤​ϕ​(s,a,b)subscriptℙℎconditionalsuperscript𝑠′𝑠𝑎𝑏subscript𝜽ℎsuperscriptsuperscript𝑠′topbold-italic-ϕ𝑠𝑎𝑏\mathbb{P}_{h}(s^{\prime}|s,a,b)=\bm{\theta}_{h}(s^{\prime})^{\top}\bm{\phi}(s,a,b) (Xie et al., 2020). We then prove that “linear MG ⊂\subset low self-play/adversarial GEC" with dGEC=O~​(H3​d)subscript𝑑GEC~𝑂superscript𝐻3𝑑d_{\mathrm{GEC}}=\widetilde{O}(H^{3}d).•Linear Mixture MG. This FOMG class admits a different type of the linear structure for the transition (Chen et al., 2022d) as ℙh​(s′|s,a,b)=𝜽h⊤​ϕ​(s,a,b,s′)subscriptℙℎconditionalsuperscript𝑠′𝑠𝑎𝑏superscriptsubscript𝜽ℎtopbold-italic-ϕ𝑠𝑎𝑏superscript𝑠′\mathbb{P}_{h}(s^{\prime}|s,a,b)=\bm{\theta}_{h}^{\top}\bm{\phi}(s,a,b,s^{\prime}) with ϕ​(s,a,b,s′)∈ℝdbold-italic-ϕ𝑠𝑎𝑏superscript𝑠′superscriptℝ𝑑\bm{\phi}(s,a,b,s^{\prime})\in\mathbb{R}^{d}. We prove that “linear mixture MG ⊂\subset low self-play/adversarial GEC" with dGEC=O~​(H3​d)subscript𝑑GEC~𝑂superscript𝐻3𝑑d_{\mathrm{GEC}}=\widetilde{O}(H^{3}d).•Low Self-Play Witness Rank. (Huang et al., 2021) defines this FOMG class for self-play by supposing that an inner product of specific vectors in ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑\mathbb{R}^{d} defined on current models can lower bound witnessed model misfit and upper bound the Bellman error with a coefficient κwitsubscript𝜅wit\kappa_{\mathrm{wit}}, which generalizes linear/linear mixture MGs.We can prove “low self-play witness rank ⊂\subset low self-play GEC" with dGEC=O~​(H3​d/κwit2)subscript𝑑GEC~𝑂superscript𝐻3𝑑subscriptsuperscript𝜅2witd_{\mathrm{GEC}}=\widetilde{O}(H^{3}d/\kappa^{2}_{\mathrm{wit}}).•α𝛼\alpha-Weakly Revealing POMG. This POMG class assumes minh⁡σS​(𝕆h)≥αsubscriptℎsubscript𝜎𝑆subscript𝕆ℎ𝛼\min_{h}\sigma_{S}(\mathbb{O}_{h})\geq\alpha where 𝕆h∈ℝ|𝒪|×|𝒮|subscript𝕆ℎsuperscriptℝ𝒪𝒮\mathbb{O}_{h}\in\mathbb{R}^{|\mathcal{O}|\times|{\mathcal{S}}|} is the matrix by 𝕆h(⋅|⋅)\mathbb{O}_{h}(\cdot|\cdot) and σSsubscript𝜎𝑆\sigma_{S} is the S𝑆S-th singular value (Liu et al., 2022c). We prove that “α𝛼\alpha-weakly revealing POMG ⊂\subset low self-play/adversarial GEC" with dGEC=O~​(H3​|𝒪|3​|𝒜|2​|ℬ|2​|𝒮|2/α2)subscript𝑑GEC~𝑂superscript𝐻3superscript𝒪3superscript𝒜2superscriptℬ2superscript𝒮2superscript𝛼2d_{\mathrm{GEC}}=\widetilde{O}(H^{3}|\mathcal{O}|^{3}|\mathcal{A}|^{2}|\mathcal{B}|^{2}|{\mathcal{S}}|^{2}/\alpha^{2}).•Decodable POMG. We propose decodable POMGs by generalizing decodable POMDPs (Efroni et al., 2022; Du et al., 2019), assuming that an unknown decoder ϕhsubscriptitalic-ϕℎ\phi_{h} recovers states from observations, i.e., ϕh​(o)=ssubscriptitalic-ϕℎ𝑜𝑠\phi_{h}(o)=s. Discussion of ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}). We briefly discuss the upper bound of the quantity ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) for FOMGs and POMGs. We refer readers to Appendix 8 for more detailed proofs. For FOMGs, according to Lemma 2 of Agarwal & Zhang (2022a), when ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is finite, p0=Unif​(ℱ)superscript𝑝0Unifℱp^{0}=\mathrm{Unif}(\mathcal{F}), then ω​(β,p0)≤log⁡|ℱ|𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0ℱ\omega(\beta,p^{0})\leq\log|\mathcal{F}| by its definition. When ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is infinite, it shows that under mild conditions, there exists a prior p0superscript𝑝0p^{0} over ℱℱ\mathcal{F}, B≥log⁡(6​B2/ϵ)𝐵6superscript𝐵2italic-ϵB\geq\log(6B^{2}/\epsilon), and ν=ϵ/(6​log⁡(6​B2/ϵ))𝜈italic-ϵ66superscript𝐵2italic-ϵ\nu=\epsilon/(6\log(6B^{2}/\epsilon)) such that ω​(β,p0)≤β​ϵ+log⁡(𝒩​(ϵ6​log⁡(B/ν)))𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0𝛽italic-ϵ𝒩italic-ϵ6𝐵𝜈\omega(\beta,p^{0})\leq\beta\epsilon+\log(\mathcal{N}(\frac{\epsilon}{6\log(B/\nu)})), where 𝒩​(ϵ)𝒩italic-ϵ\mathcal{N}(\epsilon) is the ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon-covering number w.r.t. the distanced(f,f′):=sups,a,b,h|DHe2(ℙf,h(⋅|s,a,b),ℙf∗,h(⋅|s,a,b))−DHe2(ℙf′,h(⋅|s,a,b),ℙf∗,h(⋅|s,a,b))|d(f,f^{\prime}):=\sup_{s,a,b,h}|D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbb{P}_{f,h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b),\mathbb{P}_{f^{*},h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b))-D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbb{P}_{f^{\prime},h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b),\mathbb{P}_{f^{*},h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b))|. Since we have |DHe2​(P,R)−DHe2​(Q,R)|≤22​‖P−Q‖1superscriptsubscript𝐷He2𝑃𝑅superscriptsubscript𝐷He2𝑄𝑅22subscriptnorm𝑃𝑄1|D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(P,R)-D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(Q,R)|\leq\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\|P-Q\|_{1} for any distributions P,Q𝑃𝑄P,Q, and R𝑅R, the covering number w.r.t. the distance d𝑑d can connect to the more common covering number w.r.t. the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance. Thus, the upper bound of ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) can be calculated for different cases. Additionally, for POMGs, inspired by Agarwal & Zhang (2022a), our work proves that under similar conditions, ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) with finite and infinite ℱℱ\mathcal{F} admit the same bounds as those for FOMGs. The difference is that the covering number is w.r.t. the distance d​(f,f′)=supπ,ν|DHe2​(𝐏f,Hπ,ν,𝐏f∗,Hπ,ν)−DHe2​(𝐏f′,Hπ,ν,𝐏f∗,Hπ,ν)|𝑑𝑓superscript𝑓′subscriptsupremum𝜋𝜈superscriptsubscript𝐷He2superscriptsubscript𝐏𝑓𝐻𝜋𝜈superscriptsubscript𝐏superscript𝑓𝐻𝜋𝜈superscriptsubscript𝐷He2superscriptsubscript𝐏superscript𝑓′𝐻𝜋𝜈superscriptsubscript𝐏superscript𝑓𝐻𝜋𝜈d(f,f^{\prime})=\sup_{\pi,\nu}|D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbf{P}_{f,H}^{\pi,\nu},\mathbf{P}_{f^{*},H}^{\pi,\nu})-D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(\mathbf{P}_{f^{\prime},H}^{\pi,\nu},\mathbf{P}_{f^{*},H}^{\pi,\nu})|, which further connects to the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance defined as d1​(f,f′):=supπ,ν‖𝐏f,Hπ,ν−𝐏f′,Hπ,ν‖1assignsubscript𝑑1𝑓superscript𝑓′subscriptsupremum𝜋𝜈subscriptnormsuperscriptsubscript𝐏𝑓𝐻𝜋𝜈superscriptsubscript𝐏superscript𝑓′𝐻𝜋𝜈1d_{1}(f,f^{\prime}):=\sup_{\pi,\nu}\|\mathbf{P}_{f,H}^{\pi,\nu}-\mathbf{P}_{f^{\prime},H}^{\pi,\nu}\|_{1}. Such a covering number under ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance is further analyzed in Zhan et al. (2022b). Our work gives the first detailed proof for the upper bound of ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) under the partially observable setting, which is thus of independent interest.

Next, we outline our proof sketches. Detailed proofs are deferred to Appendices 9, 10, and 11.

In this section, we present the computation of the self-play and adversarial GEC for different FOMG and POMG classes, including linear MGs, linear mixture MGs, low-witness-rank MGs, weakly revealing POMGs, and a novel POMG class, dubbed decodable POMGs, newly proposed by this work.

Note that the GEC dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}} essentially depends on ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon as shown in Definition 3.1 and Definition 4.2 such that it should be expressed as dGECϵsuperscriptsubscript𝑑GECitalic-ϵd_{\mathrm{GEC}}^{\epsilon}. For convenience, we omit such a dependence in the notation. In our main theorems, we set ϵ=1/H​Titalic-ϵ1𝐻𝑇\epsilon=1/\sqrt{HT}, and according to our derivation in this section, dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}} has an O(log(1/ϵ))=O(⋅log(HT))O(\log(1/\epsilon))=O(\cdot\log(HT)) factor which only scales logarithmically in T𝑇T.

In this subsection, we show that the linear MG is in the class of MGs with low self-play and adversarial GEC and further calculate dGECsubscript𝑑GECd_{\mathrm{GEC}} for the linear MG.

In this subsection, we discuss the linear mixture MG setting, where the transition kernel is a linear combination of some basis transition kernels and thus admits a linear structure. Specifically, we have the following definition of the linear mixture MG.

In the context of linear mixture MGs, both self-play and adversarial GEC can be calculated.

The proof of GEC bound in a linear mixture Markov game is similar to the one in the linear Markov game case. Witness rank is a complexity measure that absorbs a wide range of settings in single-agent RL (Sun et al., 2019). The work Huang et al. (2021) further studies the witness rank under the MG setting with self-play. In this subsection, we show that MGs with low self-play GEC subsumes the class of MGs with a low witness rank in the self-play setting. Since the witness rank under the adversarial setting was not investigated in prior works, we here only analyze the self-play setting.

Before presenting our main proposition, we first show the definitions of witnessed model misfit and witness rank in MGs with self-play (Huang et al., 2021).

The definition of (π˘,ν˘)˘𝜋˘𝜈(\breve{\pi},\breve{\nu}) matches the requirement for the policy pair in the self-play GEC, i.e., condition (1) in Definition 3.1. We note that condition (2) in our definition of self-play GEC is designed for Algorithm 3, which is thus a symmetric condition to (1).We define the Bellman residual asℰf,hπ,ν:=𝔼π,ν,ℙf∗​[Qf,hπ,ν​(sh,ah,bh)−𝔼sh+1∼ℙf∗,h(⋅|sh,ah,bh)​(rh​(sh,ah,bh)+Vf,h+1π,ν​(sh+1))].\displaystyle\mathcal{E}_{f,h}^{\pi,\nu}:=\mathbb{E}_{\pi,\nu,\mathbb{P}_{f^{*}}}\left[Q_{f,h}^{\pi,\nu}(s_{h},a_{h},b_{h})-\mathbb{E}_{s_{h+1}\sim\mathbb{P}_{f^{*},h}(\cdot|s_{h},a_{h},b_{h})}\left(r_{h}(s_{h},a_{h},b_{h})+V_{f,h+1}^{\pi,\nu}(s_{h+1})\right)\right].Then, we have the following definition of witness rank for MGs with self-play.

In this section, we consider a weakly revealing POMG. Based on the above definitions, we then derive the GEC bound for an α𝛼\alpha-revealing POMG in the following proposition.

To prove this proposition, we provide several supporting lemmas in Appendix 7.4.1.

We prove the former statement first. In this section, we propose a new class of POMGs, dubbed decodable POMG, by generalizing decodable POMDPs (Efroni et al., 2022; Du et al., 2019) from the single-agent setting to the multi-agent setting.

Given the decoder, we can define the transition from observation to observation as follows,ℙh​(oh+1|oh,ah,bh)subscriptℙℎconditionalsubscript𝑜ℎ1subscript𝑜ℎsubscript𝑎ℎsubscript𝑏ℎ\displaystyle\mathbb{P}_{h}(o_{h+1}{\,|\,}o_{h},a_{h},b_{h})=∑sh+1∈𝒮𝕆​(oh+1|sh+1)​ℙ​(sh+1|sh=ϕh​(oh),ah,bh).absentsubscriptsubscript𝑠ℎ1𝒮𝕆conditionalsubscript𝑜ℎ1subscript𝑠ℎ1ℙconditionalsubscript𝑠ℎ1subscript𝑠ℎsubscriptitalic-ϕℎsubscript𝑜ℎsubscript𝑎ℎsubscript𝑏ℎ\displaystyle\qquad=\sum_{s_{h+1}\in{\mathcal{S}}}\mathbb{O}(o_{h+1}{\,|\,}s_{h+1})\mathbb{P}(s_{h+1}{\,|\,}s_{h}=\phi_{h}(o_{h}),a_{h},b_{h}).

Our next proposition will show that the class of decodable POMGs is subsumed by the class of MGs with low self-play and adversarial GEC.

The proof of this proposition is largely the same as the proof of Proposition 7.9, except that the coefficient before ‖x‖1subscriptnorm𝑥1\|x\|_{1} in Lemma 7.10 and 7.11 is changed, from Sα𝑆𝛼\frac{\sqrt{S}}{\alpha} to 111, as is proved in Lemma 7.17 and Lemma 7.18. Following the proof of Proposition 7.9 and using Lemmas 7.17 and 7.18 yields our result.∎

We present the lemmas for Proposition 7.16. These lemmas are analogous to Lemma 7.10 and Lemma 7.11. For convenience in analysis, we define some notations as follows.

In this section, we discuss the upper bound of the quantity ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) for both FOMGs and POMGs. For FOMGs, the analysis can be adopted from Lemma 2 in Agarwal & Zhang (2022a). For POMGs, we give the detailed analysis to show the bound of ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}), which is the first proof for the partially observable setting.

We can adopt the result from Lemma 2 in Agarwal & Zhang (2022a) to give a bound for ω​(β,p0)𝜔𝛽superscript𝑝0\omega(\beta,p^{0}) in the context of fully observable Markov games.

To apply Proposition 8.1 in FOMGs, we can further show that|DHe2(P,R)−DHe2(Q,R)|≤2DHe2(P,R)|≤22∥P−Q∥1|D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(P,R)-D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(Q,R)|\allowbreak\leq\sqrt{2}D_{\mathrm{He}}^{2}(P,R)|\leq\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\|P-Q\|_{1} for distributions P,Q,R𝑃𝑄𝑅P,Q,R, so thatthe covering number under the distance d𝑑d can be bounded by the covering number w.r.t. the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance denoted as 𝒩1​(ϵ)subscript𝒩1italic-ϵ\mathcal{N}_{1}(\epsilon), i.e.,𝒩​(ϵ)≤𝒩1​(2​ϵ),𝒩italic-ϵsubscript𝒩12italic-ϵ\displaystyle\mathcal{N}(\epsilon)\leq\mathcal{N}_{1}(\sqrt{2}\epsilon),where 𝒩1​(ϵ)subscript𝒩1italic-ϵ\mathcal{N}_{1}(\epsilon) is defined w.r.t. the distanced1(f,f′)=sups,a,b,h∥ℙf,h(⋅|s,a,b)−ℙf′,h(⋅|s,a,b)∥1.\displaystyle d_{1}(f,f^{\prime})=\sup_{s,a,b,h}\|\mathbb{P}_{f,h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b)-\mathbb{P}_{f^{\prime},h}(\cdot{\,|\,}s,a,b)\|_{1}.The covering number under the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance is more common and is readily applicable to many problems.

In this section, we provide the detailed proof for Theorem 3.5. In particular, our proof is compatible with both the FOMG and the POMG settings. Thus, we unify both setups in a single theorem and present their proof together in this section.

To characterize the value difference under models f𝑓f and f∗superscript𝑓f^{*}, we define the following terms, which areΔ​Vfπ,∗​(s):=Vfπ,∗​(s)−Vf∗π,∗​(s),assignΔsubscriptsuperscript𝑉𝜋𝑓𝑠superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑓𝜋𝑠superscriptsubscript𝑉superscript𝑓𝜋𝑠\displaystyle\Delta The proof of Theorem 3.5 is immediate by the relation that Regsp​(T)=Reg1sp​(T)+Reg2sp​(T)superscriptRegsp𝑇superscriptsubscriptReg1sp𝑇superscriptsubscriptReg2sp𝑇\mathrm{Reg}^{\mathrm{sp}}(T)=\mathrm{Reg}_{1}^{\mathrm{sp}}(T)+\mathrm{Reg}_{2}^{\mathrm{sp}}(T). In this section, we provide the detailed proof for Theorem 4.3 and unify the proofs for both the FOMG and POMG settings together.
